<p>The meaning of <b>spirituality</b> has developed and expanded over time, and various meanings can be found alongside each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarrollO'ConnorMeakes200544_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarrollO'ConnorMeakes200544-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoenigKingCarson201236_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoenigKingCarson201236-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECobbPuchalskiRumbold2012213_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobbPuchalskiRumbold2012213-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-definition_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-definition-4">[note 1]</a></sup> Traditionally, spirituality referred to a <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religious</a> process of re-formation which "aims to recover the original shape of man",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waaijman_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waaijman-7">[note 2]</a></sup> oriented at "the <a href="/wiki/Image_of_God" title="Image of God">image of God</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000460_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000460-5">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2002[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_January_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(January_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_6-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2002[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_January_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(January_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-6'>[5]</a></sup> as exemplified by the founders and sacred texts of the religions of the world. The term was used within early <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> to refer to a life oriented toward the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Christianity)" title="Holy Spirit (Christianity)">Holy Spirit</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWongVinsky2009_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWongVinsky2009-8">[6]</a></sup> and broadened during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">Late Middle Ages</a> to include mental aspects of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>In modern times, the term both spread to other religious traditions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGorsuchMiller1999_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGorsuchMiller1999-10">[8]</a></sup> and broadened to refer to a wider range of experience, including a range of <a href="/wiki/Western_esotericism" title="Western esotericism">esoteric traditions</a> and religious traditions. Modern usages tend to refer to a subjective experience of a sacred dimension<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaucierSkrzypinska20061259_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaucierSkrzypinska20061259-11">[9]</a></sup> and the "deepest values and meanings by which people live",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldrake20071–2_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldrake20071–2-12">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffin1988_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffin1988-13">[11]</a></sup> often in a context separate from organized religious institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWongVinsky2009_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWongVinsky2009-8">[6]</a></sup> This may involve belief in a <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> realm beyond the ordinarily observable world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2014_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2014-14">[12]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Personal_growth" title="Personal growth">personal growth</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoutmanAupers2007_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoutmanAupers2007-15">[13]</a></sup> a quest for an ultimate or sacred <a href="/wiki/Meaning_of_life" title="Meaning of life">meaning</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESnyderLopez2007261_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESnyderLopez2007261-16">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Religious_experience" title="Religious experience">religious experience</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharf2000_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharf2000-17">[15]</a></sup>  or an encounter with one's own "inner dimension".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2002315_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2002315-18">[16]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The term spirit means "animating or vital principle in man and animals".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED-Spirit_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-Spirit-19">[web 1]</a></sup> It is derived from the Old French <i>espirit</i>, which comes from the Latin word <i>spiritus</i> (<a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">soul</a>, ghost, courage, vigor, breath) and is related to <i>spirare</i> (to breathe). In the <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a> the Latin word <i>spiritus</i> is used to translate the Greek <i><a href="/wiki/Pneuma" title="Pneuma">pneuma</a></i> and Hebrew <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruach" title="Ruach">ruach</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED-Spirit_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED-Spirit-19">[web 1]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "spiritual", matters "concerning the spirit", is derived from Old French <i>spirituel</i> (12c.), which is derived from Latin <i>spiritualis</i>, which comes by <i>spiritus</i> or "spirit".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED-Spiritual_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-Spiritual-20">[web 2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "spirituality" is derived from Middle French <i>spiritualité</i>, from Late Latin "spiritualitatem" (nominative spiritualitas), which is also derived from Latin <i>spiritualis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED-Spirituality_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-Spirituality-21">[web 3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There is no single, widely agreed-upon definition of spirituality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoenigKingCarson201236_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoenigKingCarson201236-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECobbPuchalskiRumbold2012213_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobbPuchalskiRumbold2012213-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-definition_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-definition-4">[note 1]</a></sup> Surveys of the definition of the term, as used in scholarly research, show a broad range of definitions with limited overlap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarrollO'ConnorMeakes200544_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarrollO'ConnorMeakes200544-1">[1]</a></sup> A survey of reviews by McCarroll each dealing with the topic of spirituality gave twenty-seven explicit definitions, among which "there was little agreement".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarrollO'ConnorMeakes200544_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarrollO'ConnorMeakes200544-1">[1]</a></sup> This impedes the systematic study of spirituality and the capacity to communicate findings meaningfully. Furthermore, many of spirituality's core features are not unique to spirituality; for example self-<a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(religion)" title="Transcendence (religion)">transcendence</a>, asceticism and the recognition of one's connection to all were regarded by the atheist <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schopenhauer" title="Arthur Schopenhauer">Arthur Schopenhauer</a> as key to ethical life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[17]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (August 2018)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/William_James" title="William James">William James</a> in his study of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Varieties_of_Religious_Experience" title="The Varieties of Religious Experience">The Varieties of Religious Experience</a></i> makes a distinction early in this lecture series that there exists "one great partition which divides the religious field. On the one side of it lies institutional, on the other personal religion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJames1901–190234_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJames1901–190234-23">[18]</a></sup> He defines "personal religion" as, "the feelings, acts, and experiences of individual men in their solitude, so far as they apprehend themselves to stand in relation to whatever they may consider the divine".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJames1901–190236_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJames1901–190236-24">[19]</a></sup> Here as well, this notion of the divine is non-sectarian and non-institutional. The divine can be found, according to William James, in spiritual spaces without a <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/godhead" title="wikt:godhead">Godhead</a>, such as "Buddhism", for instance, and he even claims that this notion of divinity is found in "modern <a href="/wiki/Transcendental_idealism" title="Transcendental idealism">transcendental idealism</a>" and in what he terms "<a href="/wiki/Ralph_Waldo_Emerson#Lifestyle_and_beliefs" title="Ralph Waldo Emerson">Emmersonianism</a>", both of which "seem to let God evaporate into abstract Ideality. Not a deity <i>in concreto</i>, not a superhuman person, but the immanent divinity in things, the essentially spiritual structure of the universe [...]".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJames1901–190237_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJames1901–190237-25">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Kees Waaijman, the traditional meaning of spirituality is a process of re-formation which "aims to recover the original shape of man, the image of God. To accomplish this, the re-formation is oriented at a mold, which represents the original shape: in <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> there is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christ" title="Christ">Christ</a>, for <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waaijman_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Waaijman-7">[note 2]</a></sup> Houtman and Aupers suggest that modern spirituality is a blend of humanistic psychology, mystical and esoteric traditions, and Eastern religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoutmanAupers2007_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoutmanAupers2007-15">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>In modern times the emphasis is on subjective experience<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaucierSkrzypinska20061259_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaucierSkrzypinska20061259-11">[9]</a></sup>  and the "deepest values and meanings by which people live",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldrake20071–2_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldrake20071–2-12">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffin1988_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffin1988-13">[11]</a></sup> incorporating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Personal_growth" title="Personal growth">personal growth or transformation</a>, usually in a context separate from organized religious institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWongVinsky2009_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWongVinsky2009-8">[6]</a></sup> Spirituality can be defined generally as an individual's search for ultimate or sacred <a href="/wiki/Meaning_of_life" title="Meaning of life">meaning and purpose</a> in life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESnyderLopez2007261_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESnyderLopez2007261-16">[14]</a></sup> Additionally it can mean to seek out or search for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Personal_growth" title="Personal growth">personal growth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religious_experience" title="Religious experience">religious experience</a>, belief in a supernatural realm or afterlife, or to make sense of one's own "inner dimension".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2014_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2014-14">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoutmanAupers2007_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoutmanAupers2007-15">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharf2000_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharf2000-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2002315_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2002315-18">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_the_meaning_of_spirituality">Development of the meaning of spirituality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Development of the meaning of spirituality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Classical.2C_medieval_and_early_modern_periods"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical,_medieval_and_early_modern_periods">Classical, medieval and early modern periods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Classical, medieval and early modern periods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bergomi detects "an enlightened form of non-religious spirituality" in <a href="/wiki/Late_antiquity" title="Late antiquity">late antiquity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Words translatable as "spirituality" first began to arise in the 5th century and only entered common use toward the end of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[22]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (April 2019)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>]</sup> In a Biblical context the term means being animated by God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000359–360_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000359–360-28">[23]</a></sup> The New Testament offers the concept of being driven by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Christianity)" title="Holy Spirit (Christianity)">Holy Spirit</a>, as opposed to living a <a href="/wiki/Personal_life" title="Personal life">life</a> in which one rejects this influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWongVinsky2009_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWongVinsky2009-8">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 11th century this meaning changed. "Spirituality" began to denote the mental aspect of life, as opposed to the material and sensual aspects of life, "the ecclesiastical sphere of light against the dark world of matter".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000360_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000360-29">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[note 3]</a></sup> In the 13th century "spirituality" acquired a social and psychological meaning. Socially it denoted the territory of the clergy: "the ecclesiastical against the temporary possessions, the ecclesiastical against the secular authority, the clerical class against the secular class".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000360–361_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000360–361-31">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[note 4]</a></sup> Psychologically, it denoted the realm of the inner life: "the purity of motives, affections, intentions, inner dispositions, the psychology of the spiritual life, the analysis of the feelings".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000361_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000361-33">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[note 5]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 17th and 18th centuries a distinction was made<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (April 2019)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> between higher and lower forms of spirituality<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>: "A spiritual man is one who is Christian 'more abundantly and deeper than others'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000361_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000361-33">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[note 6]</a></sup> The word was also associated with <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">mysticism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quietism_(Christian_philosophy)" title="Quietism (Christian philosophy)">quietism</a>, and acquired a negative meaning.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_spirituality">Modern spirituality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Modern spirituality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Western_esotericism#18th,_19th_and_early_20th_centuries" title="Western esotericism">History of Western esotericism</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a></div>
<p>Modern notions of spirituality developed throughout the 19th and 20th century, mixing Christian ideas with <a href="/wiki/Western_esotericism" title="Western esotericism">Western esoteric</a> traditions and elements of Asian, especially Indian, religions. Spirituality became increasingly disconnected from traditional religious organisations and institutions. It is sometimes associated today with philosophical, social, or political movements such as <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feminist_theology" title="Feminist theology">feminist theology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Green_politics" title="Green politics">green politics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESnyderLopez2007261–261_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESnyderLopez2007261–261-36">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transcendentalism_and_Unitarian_Universalism">Transcendentalism and Unitarian Universalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Transcendentalism and Unitarian Universalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ralph_Waldo_Emerson" title="Ralph Waldo Emerson">Ralph Waldo Emerson</a> (1803–1882) was a pioneer of the idea of spirituality as a distinct field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[28]</a></sup> He was one of the major figures in <a href="/wiki/Transcendentalism" title="Transcendentalism">Transcendentalism</a>, an early 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Christianity" title="Liberal Christianity">liberal Protestant</a> movement, which was rooted in English and German <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romanticism</a>, the Biblical criticism of <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottfried_Herder" title="Johann Gottfried Herder">Johann Gottfried Herder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Schleiermacher" title="Friedrich Schleiermacher">Friedrich Schleiermacher</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Skepticism" title="Skepticism">skepticism</a> of <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">Hume</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanford-Transcendentlism_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford-Transcendentlism-38">[web 4]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemes2014202_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemes2014202-39">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVersluis201435_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVersluis201435-40">[30]</a></sup>
The Transcendentalists emphasised an intuitive, experiential approach of religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-41">[web 5]</a></sup> Following Schleiermacher,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharf1995_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharf1995-42">[31]</a></sup> an individual's intuition of truth was taken as the criterion for truth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-41">[web 5]</a></sup> In the late 18th and early 19th century, the first translations of Hindu texts appeared, which were also read by the Transcendentalists, and influenced their thinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-41">[web 5]</a></sup> They also endorsed <a href="/wiki/Universalism" title="Universalism">universalist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarianist</a> ideas, leading to <a href="/wiki/Unitarian_Universalism" title="Unitarian Universalism">Unitarian Universalism</a>, the idea that there must be truth in other religions as well, since a loving God would redeem all living beings, not just Christians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-41">[web 5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andrews_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Andrews-43">[web 6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Theosophy.2C_anthroposophy.2C_and_the_perennial_philosophy"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Theosophy,_anthroposophy,_and_the_perennial_philosophy">Theosophy, anthroposophy, and the perennial philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Theosophy, anthroposophy, and the perennial philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Western_esotericism" title="Western esotericism">Western esotericism</a></div>
<p>A major influence on modern spirituality was the <a href="/wiki/Theosophical_Society" title="Theosophical Society">Theosophical Society</a>, which searched for 'secret teachings' in Asian religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcMahan2008_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcMahan2008-44">[32]</a></sup> It has been influential on modernist streams in several Asian religions, notably <a href="/wiki/Neo-Vedanta" title="Neo-Vedanta">Neo-Vedanta</a>, the revival of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theravada_Buddhism" title="Theravada Buddhism">Theravada Buddhism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_modernism" title="Buddhist modernism">Buddhist modernism</a>, which have taken over modern western notions of <a href="/wiki/Religious_experience" title="Religious experience">personal experience</a> and <a href="/wiki/Universalism" title="Universalism">universalism</a> and integrated them in their religious concepts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcMahan2008_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcMahan2008-44">[32]</a></sup> A second, related influence was <a href="/wiki/Anthroposophy" title="Anthroposophy">Anthroposophy</a>, whose founder, <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Steiner" title="Rudolf Steiner">Rudolf Steiner</a>, was particularly interested in developing a genuine Western spirituality, and in the ways that such a spirituality could transform practical institutions such as <a href="/wiki/Waldorf_education" title="Waldorf education">education</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biodynamic_agriculture" title="Biodynamic agriculture">agriculture</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthroposophical_medicine" title="Anthroposophical medicine">medicine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[34]</a></sup> More independently, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiritual_science" title="Spiritual science">spiritual science</a> of <a href="/wiki/Martinus_Thomsen" title="Martinus Thomsen">Martinus</a> was an influence, especially in Scandinavia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>The influence of <a href="/wiki/Enlightenment_(spiritual)" title="Enlightenment (spiritual)">Asian traditions</a> on western modern spirituality was also furthered by the <a href="/wiki/Perennial_philosophy" title="Perennial philosophy">perennial philosophy</a>, whose main proponent <a href="/wiki/Aldous_Huxley" title="Aldous Huxley">Aldous Huxley</a> was deeply influenced by <a href="/wiki/Swami_Vivekananda" title="Swami Vivekananda">Swami Vivekananda's</a> <a href="/wiki/Neo-Vedanta" title="Neo-Vedanta">Neo-Vedanta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Universalism" title="Universalism">universalism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoy2003_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoy2003-48">[36]</a></sup> and the spread of social welfare, education and mass travel after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Neo-Vedanta">Neo-Vedanta</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Neo-Vedanta">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neo-Vedanta" title="Neo-Vedanta">Neo-Vedanta</a></div>
<p>An important influence on western spirituality was <a href="/wiki/Neo-Vedanta" title="Neo-Vedanta">Neo-Vedanta</a>, also called <i>neo-Hinduism</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing200293_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing200293-49">[37]</a></sup> and <i>Hindu Universalism</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[web 7]</a></sup> a modern interpretation of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> which developed in response to western <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orientalism" title="Orientalism">orientalism</a>. It aims to present Hinduism as a "homogenized ideal of Hinduism"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYelle2012338_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYelle2012338-51">[38]</a></sup> with Advaita Vedanta as its central doctrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing2002135_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing2002135-52">[39]</a></sup> Due to the colonisation of Asia by the western world, since the 19th century an exchange of ideas has been taking place between the western world and Asia, which also influenced western religiosity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcMahan2008_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcMahan2008-44">[32]</a></sup> Unitarianism, and the idea of Universalism, was brought to India by missionaries, and had a major influence on neo-Hinduism via <a href="/wiki/Ram_Mohan_Roy" title="Ram Mohan Roy">Ram Mohan Roy</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Brahmo_Samaj" title="Brahmo Samaj">Brahmo Samaj</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brahmoism" title="Brahmoism">Brahmoism</a>. Roy attempted to modernise and reform Hinduism, from the idea of Universalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing2002_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing2002-53">[40]</a></sup> This universalism was further popularised, and brought back to the west as neo-Vedanta, by <a href="/wiki/Swami_Vivekananda" title="Swami Vivekananda">Swami Vivekananda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing2002_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing2002-53">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id=".22Spiritual_but_not_religious.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='"Spiritual_but_not_religious"'>"Spiritual but not religious"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title='Edit section: "Spiritual but not religious"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spiritual_but_not_religious" title="Spiritual but not religious">Spiritual but not religious</a></div>
<p>After the Second World War, spirituality and theistic religion became increasingly disconnected,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000361_33-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000361-33">[26]</a></sup> and spirituality became more oriented on subjective experience, instead of "attempts to place the self within a broader ontological context".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaucierSkrzypinska20061259_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaucierSkrzypinska20061259-11">[9]</a></sup> A new discourse developed, in which (humanistic) psychology, mystical and esoteric traditions and eastern religions are being blended, to reach the <a href="/wiki/True_self_and_false_self" title="True self and false self">true self</a> by <a href="/wiki/Self-disclosure" title="Self-disclosure">self-disclosure</a>, free expression, and meditation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoutmanAupers2007_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoutmanAupers2007-15">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>The distinction between the spiritual and the religious became more common in the popular mind during the late 20th century with the rise of <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secularism</a> and the advent of the <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a> movement.  Authors such as Chris Griscom and <a href="/wiki/Shirley_MacLaine" title="Shirley MacLaine">Shirley MacLaine</a> explored it in numerous ways in their books. <a href="/wiki/Paul_Heelas" title="Paul Heelas">Paul Heelas</a> noted the development within New Age circles of what he called "seminar spirituality":<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[41]</a></sup> structured offerings complementing <a href="/wiki/Consumerism" title="Consumerism">consumer</a> choice with spiritual options.
</p><p>Among other factors, declining membership of organized religions and the growth of secularism in the <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">western world</a> have given rise to this broader view of spirituality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[42]</a></sup> The term "spiritual" is now frequently used in contexts in which the term "religious" was formerly employed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGorsuchMiller1999_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGorsuchMiller1999-10">[8]</a></sup> Both theists and atheists have criticized this development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_spirituality">Traditional spirituality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Traditional spirituality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abrahamic_faiths">Abrahamic faiths</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Abrahamic faiths">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Judaism">Judaism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Judaism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Spirituality in <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> may involve practices of <a href="/wiki/Jewish_ethics" title="Jewish ethics">Jewish ethics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jewish_prayer" title="Jewish prayer">Jewish prayer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jewish_meditation" title="Jewish meditation">Jewish meditation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shabbat" title="Shabbat">Shabbat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jewish_holidays" title="Jewish holidays">holiday</a> observance, <a href="/wiki/Torah_study" title="Torah study">Torah study</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kashrut" title="Kashrut">dietary laws</a>, <a href="/wiki/Repentance_in_Judaism" title="Repentance in Judaism">teshuvah</a>, and other practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-58">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESonsino2002[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_January_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(January_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_59-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESonsino2002[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_January_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(January_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-59'>[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[web 8]</a></sup> It may involve practices ordained by <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">halakhah</a> or other practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-58">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kabbalah" title="Kabbalah">Kabbalah</a> (literally "receiving") is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Esoteric" title="Esoteric">esoteric</a> method, discipline and school of thought of Judaism. Kabbalah is a set of esoteric teachings meant to explain the relationship between an unchanging, eternal and mysterious <a href="/wiki/Ein_Sof" title="Ein Sof">Ein Sof</a> (no end) and the mortal and finite universe (his creation). Interpretations of Kabbalistic spirituality are found within <a href="/wiki/Hasidic_Judaism" title="Hasidic Judaism">Hasidic Judaism</a>, a branch of <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Judaism" title="Orthodox Judaism">Orthodox Judaism</a> founded in 18th-century Eastern Europe by Rabbi Israel <a href="/wiki/Baal_Shem_Tov" title="Baal Shem Tov">Baal Shem Tov</a>. Hasidism often emphasizes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divine_immanence" title="Divine immanence">Immanent</a> Divine presence and focuses on emotion, <a href="/wiki/Devekut" title="Devekut">fervour</a>, and the figure of the <a href="/wiki/Tzadik" title="Tzadik">Tzadik</a>. This movement included an elite ideal of nullification to paradoxical Divine <a href="/wiki/Panentheism" title="Panentheism">Panentheism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Musar_movement" title="Musar movement">Musar movement</a> is a Jewish spiritual movement that has focused on developing character traits such as <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Humility" title="Humility">humility</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_theology_of_love" title="Jewish theology of love">love</a>.  The Musar movement, first founded in the 19th century by <a href="/wiki/Israel_Salanter" title="Israel Salanter">Israel Salanter</a> and developed in the 21st century by <a href="/wiki/Alan_Morinis" title="Alan Morinis">Alan Morinis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ira_F._Stone" title="Ira F. Stone">Ira F. Stone</a>, has encouraged spiritual practices of <a href="/wiki/Jewish_meditation" title="Jewish meditation">Jewish meditation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jewish_prayer" title="Jewish prayer">Jewish prayer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jewish_ethics" title="Jewish ethics">Jewish ethics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tzedakah" title="Tzedakah">tzedakah</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teshuvah" title="Teshuvah">teshuvah</a>, and the study of <a href="/wiki/Musar_literature" title="Musar literature">musar (ethical) literature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Reform_Judaism" title="Reform Judaism">Reform Judaism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Judaism" title="Conservative Judaism">Conservative Judaism</a> have often emphasized the spirituality of <a href="/wiki/Jewish_ethics" title="Jewish ethics">Jewish ethics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tikkun_olam" title="Tikkun olam">tikkun olam</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorff201849_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorff201849-64">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2018[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_January_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(January_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_65-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2018[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_January_2022]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(January_2022)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-65'>[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jewish_feminism" title="Jewish feminism">feminist spirituality</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorff201849,_151_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorff201849,_151-66">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan201353_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan201353-67">[53]</a></sup> Jewish prayer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorff201869–70_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorff201869–70-68">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESonsino200272–92_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESonsino200272–92-69">[55]</a></sup> Torah study,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorff201891_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorff201891-70">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESonsino200256–59_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESonsino200256–59-71">[57]</a></sup> ritual,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan201350_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan201350-72">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESonsino2002112–129_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESonsino2002112–129-73">[59]</a></sup> and musar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Catholic_spirituality" title="Catholic spirituality">Catholic spirituality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christian_mysticism" title="Christian mysticism">Christian mysticism</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px .ambox-delete{border-left:10px solid #b32424}.mw-parser-output .ambox-content{border-left:10px solid #f28500}.mw-parser-output .ambox-style{border-left:10px solid #fc3}.mw-parser-output .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2018</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Risen_Christ_appears_to_his_Mother_by_Daniele_Monteleone.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="557" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Risen_Christ_appears_to_his_Mother_by_Daniele_Monteleone.jpg/220px-Risen_Christ_appears_to_his_Mother_by_Daniele_Monteleone.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Risen_Christ_appears_to_his_Mother_by_Daniele_Monteleone.jpg/330px-Risen_Christ_appears_to_his_Mother_by_Daniele_Monteleone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Risen_Christ_appears_to_his_Mother_by_Daniele_Monteleone.jpg/440px-Risen_Christ_appears_to_his_Mother_by_Daniele_Monteleone.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Risen_Christ_appears_to_his_Mother_by_Daniele_Monteleone.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Union with Christ is the purpose of Christian mysticism.</div></div></div>
<p>Catholic spirituality is the spiritual practice of living out a personal <a href="/wiki/Faith_in_Christianity#Roman_Catholicism" title="Faith in Christianity">act of faith</a> (<i>fides qua creditur</i>) following the acceptance of <a href="/wiki/Credo" title="Credo">faith</a> (<i>fides quae creditur</i>). Although all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholics" title="Catholics">Catholics</a> are expected to pray together at <a href="/wiki/Mass_(liturgy)" title="Mass (liturgy)">Mass</a>, there are many different forms of spirituality and private prayer which have developed over the centuries. Each of the major <a href="/wiki/Religious_order_(Catholic)" title="Religious order (Catholic)">religious orders</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Laity" title="Laity">lay</a> groupings have their own unique spirituality – its own way of approaching God in prayer and in living out the <a href="/wiki/Gospel" title="Gospel">Gospel</a>.
</p><p>Christian mysticism refers to the development of <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">mystical practices and theory</a> within <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>. It has often been connected to <a href="/wiki/Mystical_theology" title="Mystical theology">mystical theology</a>, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox</a> traditions. The attributes and means by which Christian mysticism is studied and practiced are varied and range from <a href="/wiki/Religious_ecstasy" title="Religious ecstasy">ecstatic</a> visions of the soul's <a href="/wiki/Bridal_theology" title="Bridal theology">mystical union with God</a> to simple prayerful <a href="/wiki/Contemplation" title="Contemplation">contemplation</a> of Holy Scripture (i.e., <i><a href="/wiki/Lectio_Divina" title="Lectio Divina">Lectio Divina</a></i>).
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Progressive_Christianity" title="Progressive Christianity">Progressive Christianity</a> is a contemporary movement which seeks to remove the supernatural claims of the faith and replace them with a post-critical understanding of biblical spirituality based on historical and scientific research. It focuses on the lived experience of spirituality over historical dogmatic claims, and accepts that the faith is both true and a human construction, and that spiritual experiences are psychologically and neurally real and useful.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">Islam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Islam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>An inner spiritual struggle and an outer physical struggle are two commonly accepted meanings of the Arabic word <i><a href="/wiki/Jihad" title="Jihad">jihad</a></i>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan201087_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan201087-75">[61]</a></sup> The "greater jihad" is the inner struggle by a believer to fulfill his religious duties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan201087_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan201087-75">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCjihad_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCjihad-76">[web 9]</a></sup> This non-violent meaning is stressed by both Muslim<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[62]</a></sup> and non-Muslim<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[63]</a></sup> authors.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Al-Khatib_al-Baghdadi" title="Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi">Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi</a>, an 11th-century Islamic scholar, referenced a statement by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahaba" title="Sahaba">companion of Muhammad</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jabir_ibn_Abd-Allah" title="Jabir ibn Abd-Allah">Jabir ibn Abd-Allah</a>:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Prophet ... returned from one of his battles, and thereupon told us, 'You have arrived with an excellent arrival, you have come from the Lesser Jihad to the Greater Jihad – the striving of a servant (of <a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a>) against his desires (holy war)."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (March 2011)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCjihad_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBCjihad-76">[web 9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[note 7]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Sufism">Sufism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Sufism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a></div>
<p>The best known form of Islamic mystic spirituality is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> tradition (famous through <a href="/wiki/Rumi" title="Rumi">Rumi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hafiz_Shirazi" title="Hafiz Shirazi">Hafiz</a>) in which a <a href="/wiki/Sheikh" title="Sheikh">Sheikh</a> or <i><a href="/wiki/Pir_(Sufism)" title="Pir (Sufism)">pir</a></i> transmits spiritual discipline to students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sufism or <span title="DIN 31635 Arabic (Arabic language) transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">taṣawwuf</i></span> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">تصوّف</span>) is defined by its adherents as the inner, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_mysticism" title="Islamic mysticism">mystical</a> dimension of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Godlas_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Godlas-82">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[68]</a></sup> A practitioner of this tradition is generally known as a <i><span title="DIN 31635 Arabic (Arabic language) transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">ṣūfī</i></span></i> (<span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">صُوفِيّ</span></span>). Sufis believe they are practicing <a href="/wiki/Ihsan" title="Ihsan">ihsan</a> (perfection of worship) as revealed by <a href="/wiki/Gabriel" title="Gabriel">Gabriel</a> to <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>,
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Worship and serve <a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a> as you are seeing Him and while you see Him not yet truly He sees you.</p></blockquote>
<p>Sufis consider themselves as the original true proponents of this pure original form of Islam. They are strong adherents to the principal of tolerance, peace and against any form of violence. The Sufi have suffered severe persecution by more rigid and fundamentalist groups such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wahhabi" title="Wahhabi">Wahhabi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salafi_movement" title="Salafi movement">Salafi movement</a>. In 1843 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Senussi" title="Senussi">Senussi</a> Sufi were forced to flee Mecca and Medina and head to Sudan and Libya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FirstDynasty_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FirstDynasty-85">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Classical Sufi scholars have defined Sufism as "a science whose objective is the reparation of the heart and turning it away from all else but God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[70]</a></sup> Alternatively, in the words of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darqawa" title="Darqawa">Darqawi</a> Sufi teacher <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Ajiba" title="Ahmad ibn Ajiba">Ahmad ibn Ajiba</a>, "a science through which one can know how to travel into the <a href="/wiki/Divine_presence" title="Divine presence">presence of the Divine</a>, purify one's inner self from filth, and beautify it with a variety of praiseworthy traits".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_religions">Indian religions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Indian religions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jainism">Jainism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Jainism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, traditionally known as Jain Dharma, is an ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_religion" title="Indian religion">Indian religion</a>. The three main pillars of Jainism are <a href="/wiki/Ahimsa_in_Jainism" title="Ahimsa in Jainism">ahiṃsā</a> (non-violence), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anek%C4%81ntav%C4%81da" title="Anekāntavāda">anekāntavāda</a> (non-absolutism), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aparigraha" title="Aparigraha">aparigraha</a> (non-attachment). Jains take five main vows: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahi%E1%B9%83s%C4%81" title="Ahiṃsā">ahiṃsā</a> (non-violence), <a href="/wiki/Satya" title="Satya">satya</a> (truth), <a href="/wiki/Achourya" title="Achourya">asteya</a> (not stealing), <a href="/wiki/Brahmacharya" title="Brahmacharya">brahmacharya</a> (sexual continence), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aparigraha" title="Aparigraha">aparigraha</a> (non-possessiveness). These principles have affected Jain culture in many ways, such as leading to a predominantly vegetarian lifestyle. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasparopagraho_J%C4%ABv%C4%81n%C4%81m" title="Parasparopagraho Jīvānām">Parasparopagraho jīvānām</a> (the function of souls is to help one another) is the faith's motto and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%E1%B9%86am%C5%8Dk%C4%81ra_mantra" title="Ṇamōkāra mantra">Ṇamōkāra mantra</a> is its most common and basic prayer.
</p><p>Jainism traces its spiritual ideas and history through a succession of twenty-four leaders or <a href="/wiki/Tirthankara" title="Tirthankara">Tirthankaras</a>, with the first in the current time cycle being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rishabhadeva" title="Rishabhadeva">Rishabhadeva</a>, whom the tradition holds to have lived millions of years ago; the twenty-third tirthankara <a href="/wiki/Parshvanatha" title="Parshvanatha">Parshvanatha</a>, whom historians date to 9th century BCE; and the twenty-fourth tirthankara, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81v%C4%ABra" title="Mahāvīra">Mahavira</a> around 600 BCE. Jainism is considered to be an eternal <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">dharma</a> with the tirthankaras guiding every time cycle of the <a href="/wiki/Jain_cosmology" title="Jain cosmology">cosmology</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Buddhism">Buddhism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Buddhism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a></div>
<p>Buddhist practices are known as <a href="/wiki/Bhavana" title="Bhavana">Bhavana</a>, which literally means "development" or "cultivating"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[72]</a></sup> or "producing"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PED_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-PED-89">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWD_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-MWD-90">[74]</a></sup> in the sense of "calling into existence."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENyanatiloka198067_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENyanatiloka198067-91">[75]</a></sup> It is an important concept in Buddhist <a href="/wiki/Praxis_(process)" title="Praxis (process)">praxis</a> (<i>Patipatti</i>). The word <i>bhavana</i> normally appears in conjunction with another word forming a compound phrase such as <i>citta-bhavana</i> (the development or cultivation of the heart/mind) or <i>metta-bhavana</i> (the development/cultivation of loving kindness). When used on its own <i>bhavana</i> signifies 'spiritual cultivation' generally.
</p><p>Various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist_Paths_to_liberation" title="Buddhist Paths to liberation">Buddhist Paths to liberation</a> developed throughout the ages. Best-known is the <a href="/wiki/Noble_Eightfold_Path" title="Noble Eightfold Path">Noble Eightfold Path</a>, but others include <a href="/wiki/Bh%C5%ABmi_(Buddhism)" title="Bhūmi (Buddhism)">the Bodhisattva Path</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lamrim" title="Lamrim">Lamrim</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hinduism">Hinduism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Hinduism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Hinduism has no traditional ecclesiastical order, no centralized religious authorities, no governing body, no prophet(s) nor any binding holy book; Hindus can choose to be polytheistic, pantheistic, monistic, or atheistic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[76]</a></sup> Within this diffuse and open structure, spirituality in Hindu philosophy is an individual experience, and referred to as ksaitrajña (Sanskrit: क्षैत्रज्ञ<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[77]</a></sup>). It defines spiritual practice as one's journey towards <a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">moksha</a>, awareness of self, the discovery of higher truths, true nature of reality, and a consciousness that is liberated and content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gf_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-gf-95">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Four_paths">Four paths</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Four paths">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a></div>
