//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Raja_Ravi_Varma_-_Sankaracharya.jpg/214px-Raja_Ravi_Varma_-_Sankaracharya.jpg 2x" width="107"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Jñāna marga</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:103px;max-width:103px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:151px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Russian_Hare_Krishnas_singing_on_the_street.jpg"><img alt="Bhakti marga" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Russian_Hare_Krishnas_singing_on_the_street.jpg/101px-Russian_Hare_Krishnas_singing_on_the_street.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Russian_Hare_Krishnas_singing_on_the_street.jpg/152px-Russian_Hare_Krishnas_singing_on_the_street.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Russian_Hare_Krishnas_singing_on_the_street.jpg/202px-Russian_Hare_Krishnas_singing_on_the_street.jpg 2x" width="101"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Bhakti marga</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:103px;max-width:103px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:151px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:People_of_Varanasi_006.jpg"><img alt="Rāja marga" data-file-height="3872" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/People_of_Varanasi_006.jpg/101px-People_of_Varanasi_006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/People_of_Varanasi_006.jpg/152px-People_of_Varanasi_006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/People_of_Varanasi_006.jpg/202px-People_of_Varanasi_006.jpg 2x" width="101"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Rāja marga</div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Three of four paths of spirituality in Hinduism</div></div></div></div>
<p>Traditionally, Hinduism identifies three <i>mārga</i> (ways)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[note 8]</a></sup> of spiritual practice,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[81]</a></sup> namely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jnana" title="Jnana">Jñāna</a>(ज्ञान), the way of knowledge; <a href="/wiki/Bhakti" title="Bhakti">Bhakti</a>, the way of devotion; and <a href="/wiki/Karma_yoga" title="Karma yoga">Karma yoga</a>, the way of selfless action. In the 19th century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vivekananda" title="Vivekananda">Vivekananda</a>, in his <a href="/wiki/Neo-Vedanta" title="Neo-Vedanta">neo-Vedanta</a> synthesis of Hinduism, added <a href="/wiki/R%C4%81ja_yoga" title="Rāja yoga">Rāja yoga</a>, the way of contemplation and meditation, as a fourth way, calling all of them "yoga".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[note 9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jñāna marga is a path often assisted by a <i>guru</i> (teacher) in one's spiritual practice.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeuerstein2003"Chapter_55"_102-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeuerstein2003"Chapter_55"-102'>[84]</a></sup> Bhakti marga is a path of faith and devotion to deity or deities; the spiritual practice often includes chanting, singing and music – such as in <i><a href="/wiki/Kirtan" title="Kirtan">kirtans</a></i> – in front of idols, or images of one or more deity, or a devotional symbol of the holy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[85]</a></sup> Karma marga is the path of one's work, where diligent practical work or <i>vartta</i> (Sanskrit: वार्त्ता, profession) becomes in itself a spiritual practice, and work in daily life is perfected as a form of spiritual liberation and not for its material rewards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[note 10]</a></sup> Rāja marga is the path of cultivating necessary virtues, self-discipline, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tapas_(Sanskrit)" title="Tapas (Sanskrit)">tapas</a></i> (meditation), contemplation and self-reflection sometimes with isolation and renunciation of the world, to a pinnacle state called <i><a href="/wiki/Samadhi" title="Samadhi">samādhi</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[89]</a></sup> This state of <i>samādhi</i> has been compared to peak experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a rigorous debate in Indian literature on relative merits of these theoretical spiritual practices. For example, <a href="/wiki/Chandogya_Upanishad" title="Chandogya Upanishad">Chandogyopanishad</a> suggests that those who engage in ritualistic offerings to gods and priests will fail in their spiritual practice, while those who engage in <i>tapas</i> will succeed; <a href="/wiki/Shvetashvatara_Upanishad" title="Shvetashvatara Upanishad">Svetasvataropanishad</a> suggests that a successful spiritual practice requires a longing for truth, but warns of becoming 'false ascetic' who go through the mechanics of spiritual practice without meditating on the nature of Self and universal Truths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crp_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-crp-110">[91]</a></sup> In the practice of Hinduism, suggest modern era scholars such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vivekananda" title="Vivekananda">Vivekananda</a>, the choice between the paths is up to the individual and a person's proclivities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gf_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-gf-95">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[92]</a></sup> Other scholars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[93]</a></sup> suggest that these Hindu spiritual practices are not mutually exclusive, but overlapping. These four paths of spirituality are also known in Hinduism outside India, such as in <a href="/wiki/Balinese_Hinduism" title="Balinese Hinduism">Balinese Hinduism</a>, where it is called <i>Chatur Marga</i> (literally: four paths).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Schools_and_spirituality">Schools and spirituality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Schools and spirituality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Different schools of Hinduism encourage different spiritual practices. In <a href="/wiki/Tantra#Hinduism" title="Tantra">Tantric</a> school for example, the spiritual practice has been referred to as <i><a href="/wiki/S%C4%81dhan%C4%81" title="Sādhanā">sādhanā</a></i>. It involves initiation into the school, undergoing rituals, and achieving moksha liberation by experiencing union of cosmic polarities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[95]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Society_for_Krishna_Consciousness" title="International Society for Krishna Consciousness">Hare Krishna</a> school emphasizes bhakti yoga as spiritual practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[96]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Advaita_Vedanta" title="Advaita Vedanta">Advaita Vedanta</a> school, the spiritual practice emphasizes jñāna yoga in stages: samnyasa (cultivate virtues), sravana (hear, study), manana (reflect) and dhyana (nididhyasana, contemplate).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sikhism">Sikhism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Sikhism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raja_Fateh_Singh_Ahluwalia_of_Kapurthala.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1792" data-file-width="2128" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Raja_Fateh_Singh_Ahluwalia_of_Kapurthala.jpg/220px-Raja_Fateh_Singh_Ahluwalia_of_Kapurthala.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Raja_Fateh_Singh_Ahluwalia_of_Kapurthala.jpg/330px-Raja_Fateh_Singh_Ahluwalia_of_Kapurthala.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Raja_Fateh_Singh_Ahluwalia_of_Kapurthala.jpg/440px-Raja_Fateh_Singh_Ahluwalia_of_Kapurthala.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raja_Fateh_Singh_Ahluwalia_of_Kapurthala.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 18th Century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sikh" title="Sikh">Sikh</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sikh_raj" title="Sikh raj">Raja</a></div></div></div>
<p>Sikhism considers spiritual life and secular life to be intertwined:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kamala_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kamala-117">[98]</a></sup> "In the Sikh Weltanschauung...the temporal world is part of the Infinite Reality and partakes of its characteristics."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nikky_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nikky-118">[99]</a></sup> Guru Nanak described living an "active, creative, and practical life" of "truthfulness, fidelity, self-control and purity" as being higher than a purely contemplative life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marwha_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marwha-119">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 6th Sikh Guru <a href="/wiki/Guru_Hargobind" title="Guru Hargobind">Guru Hargobind</a> re-affirmed that the political/temporal (Miri) and spiritual (Piri) realms are mutually coexistent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marty-120">[101]</a></sup>  According to the 9th Sikh Guru, Tegh Bahadhur, the ideal Sikh should have both <a href="/wiki/Shakti" title="Shakti">Shakti</a> (power that resides in the temporal), and <a href="/wiki/Bhakti" title="Bhakti">Bhakti</a> (spiritual meditative qualities). This was developed into the concept of the Saint Soldier by the 10th Sikh Guru, Gobind Singh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gandhi_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gandhi-121">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a>, the goal is to attain the "attendant balance of separation-fusion, self-other, action-inaction, attachment-detachment, in the course of daily life",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandair_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mandair-122">[103]</a></sup> the polar opposite to a self-centered existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandair_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mandair-122">[103]</a></sup> Nanak talks further about the <a href="/wiki/Ik_Onkar" title="Ik Onkar">one God</a> or <a href="/wiki/Akal_(Sikh_term)" title="Akal (Sikh term)">akal</a> (timelessness) that permeates all life<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[104]</a></sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalsi_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kalsi-125">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[107]</a></sup> and which must be seen with 'the inward eye', or the 'heart', of a human being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a> there is no <a href="/wiki/Dogma" title="Dogma">dogma</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[109]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">priests</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monastic" title="Monastic">monastics</a> or <a href="/wiki/Yogi" title="Yogi">yogis</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="African_spirituality">African spirituality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: African spirituality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_African_religion" title="Traditional African religion">Traditional African religion</a></div>
<p>In some African contexts,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (November 2018)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> spirituality is considered a belief system that guides the welfare of society and the people therein, and eradicates sources of unhappiness occasioned by evil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[110]</a></sup>
In traditional society prior to colonization and extensive introduction to Christianity or Islam, religion was the strongest element in society influencing the thinking and actions of the people.  Hence spirituality was a sub-domain of religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[111]</a></sup> Despite the rapid social, economic and political changes of the last century, traditional religion remains the essential background for many African people. And that religion is a communal given, not an individual choice. Religion gives all of life its meaning and provides ground for action. Each person is "a living creed of his religion." There is no concern for spiritual matters apart from ones physical and communal life. Life continues after death but remains focused on pragmatic family and community matters.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_spirituality">Contemporary spirituality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Contemporary spirituality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a></div>
<p>The term <i>spiritual</i> has frequently become used in contexts in which the term <i>religious</i> was formerly employed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGorsuchMiller1999_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGorsuchMiller1999-10">[8]</a></sup> Contemporary spirituality is also called "post-traditional spirituality" and "<a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a> spirituality".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOtterlooAupersHoutman2012239–240_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOtterlooAupersHoutman2012239–240-131">[112]</a></sup> Hanegraaf makes a distinction between two "New Age" movements: New Age in a restricted sense, which originated primarily in mid-twentieth century England and had its roots in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theosophy_(Blavatskian)" title="Theosophy (Blavatskian)">Theosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anthroposophy" title="Anthroposophy">anthroposophy</a>, and "New Age" in a general sense, which emerged in the later 1970s
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>when increasing numbers of people ... began to perceive a broad similarity between a wide variety of "alternative ideas" and pursuits, and started to think of them as part of one "movement"".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199697_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff199697-132">[113]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Those who speak of spirituality outside of religion often define themselves as <i><a href="/wiki/Spiritual_but_not_religious" title="Spiritual but not religious">spiritual but not religious</a></i> and generally believe in the existence of different "spiritual paths", emphasizing the importance of finding one's own individual path to spirituality. According to one 2005 poll, about 24% of the United States population identifies itself as "spiritual but not religious".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[web 10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Lockwood draws attention to the variety of spiritual experience in the contemporary <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">West</a>:
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The new Western spiritual landscape, characterised by <a href="/wiki/Consumerism" title="Consumerism">consumerism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Choice" title="Choice">choice</a> abundance, is scattered with novel religious manifestations based in psychology and the <a href="/wiki/Human_Potential_Movement" title="Human Potential Movement">Human Potential Movement</a>, each offering participants a pathway to the Self.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Modern spirituality centers on the <a href="/wiki/Worldview" title="Worldview">"deepest values and meanings by which people live"</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheldrake_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sheldrake-135">[115]</a></sup> It often embraces the idea of an ultimate or an alleged immaterial <a href="/wiki/Reality" title="Reality">reality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[116]</a></sup> It envisions an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Involution_(philosophy)" title="Involution (philosophy)">inner path</a> enabling a person to discover the <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essence</a> of his or her <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Being" title="Being">being</a>.
</p><p>Not all modern notions of spirituality embrace transcendental ideas. <a href="/wiki/Secular_spirituality" title="Secular spirituality">Secular spirituality</a> emphasizes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humanistic" title="Humanistic">humanistic</a> ideas on <a href="/wiki/Moral_character" title="Moral character">moral character</a> (qualities such as love, <a href="/wiki/Compassion" title="Compassion">compassion</a>, patience, tolerance, forgiveness, contentment, responsibility, harmony, and a concern for others).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dalai_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dalai-137">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 22">: 22 </span></sup> These are aspects of <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_experience" title="Human experience">human experience</a> which go beyond a purely materialist view of the world without necessarily accepting belief in a supernatural reality or any divine being. Nevertheless, many humanists (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Paul_Sartre" title="Jean-Paul Sartre">Jean-Paul Sartre</a>) who clearly value the non-material, communal, and virtuous aspects of life reject this usage of the term "spirituality" as being overly-broad (i.e. it effectively amounts to saying "everything and anything that is good and virtuous is <i>necessarily</i> spiritual").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2011_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2011-138">[118]</a></sup> In 1930 Russell, a self-described agnostic renowned as an atheist, wrote "... one's ego is no very large part of the world. The man who can centre his thoughts and hopes upon something transcending self can find a certain peace in the ordinary troubles of life which is impossible to the pure egoist."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[119]</a></sup>
Similarly, Aristotle – one of the first known Western thinkers to demonstrate that morality, virtue and goodness can be derived without appealing to supernatural forces – argued that "men create Gods in their own image" (not the other way around). Moreover, theistic and atheistic critics alike dismiss the need for the "secular spirituality" label on the basis that it appears to be nothing more than <a href="/wiki/Obscurantism" title="Obscurantism">obscurantism</a> in that:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul><li>the term "spirit" is commonly taken as denoting the existence of unseen / otherworldly / life-giving forces; and</li>
<li>words such as "morality", "<a href="/wiki/Philanthropy" title="Philanthropy">philanthropy</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanism</a>" already efficiently and succinctly describe the prosocial-orientation and civility that the phrase "<a href="/wiki/Secular_spirituality" title="Secular spirituality">secular spirituality</a>" is meant to convey but without risking confusion that one is referring to something supernatural.</li></ul>
<p>Although personal well-being, both physical and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emotional_well-being" title="Emotional well-being">psychological</a>, is said<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (September 2019)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> to be an important aspect of modern spirituality, this does not imply spirituality is <i>essential</i> to achieving happiness (e.g. <a class="external text" href="http://pcl.missouri.edu/jeff/sites/pcl.missouri.edu.jeff/files/Diener.pdf" rel="nofollow">see</a>).  Free-thinkers who reject notions that the numinous/non-material is important to living well can be just as happy as more spiritually-oriented individuals (<a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20171023010658/http://www.scienceonreligion.org/index.php/news-research/research-updates/306-does-spirituality-help-well-being-or-do-we-just-need-to-be-good-to-each-other" rel="nofollow">see</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[120]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (September 2019)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Contemporary proponents of spirituality may suggest that spirituality develops <a href="/wiki/Inner_peace" title="Inner peace">inner peace</a> and forms a foundation for <a href="/wiki/Happiness" title="Happiness">happiness</a>. For example, <a href="/wiki/Meditation" title="Meditation">meditation</a> and similar practices are suggested to help the practitioner cultivate her/his <a href="/wiki/Introspection" title="Introspection">inner life</a> and character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[121]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (June 2012)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[122]</a></sup> Ellison and Fan (2008) assert that spirituality <i>causes</i> a wide array of positive health outcomes, including "morale, happiness, and life satisfaction.".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[123]</a></sup> However, Schuurmans-Stekhoven (2013) actively attempted to replicate this research and found more "mixed" results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2013_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2013-144">[124]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (September 2019)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>]</sup> Nevertheless, spirituality has played a central role in some <a href="/wiki/Self-help" title="Self-help">self-help</a> movements such as <a href="/wiki/Alcoholics_Anonymous" title="Alcoholics Anonymous">Alcoholics Anonymous</a>:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> if an alcoholic failed to perfect and enlarge his spiritual life through work and self-sacrifice for others, he could not survive the certain trials and low spots ahead<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anonymous2009_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anonymous2009-145">[125]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Such spiritually-informed treatment approaches have been challenged as <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscience</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spiritual_experience">Spiritual experience</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Spiritual experience">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religious_experience" title="Religious experience">Religious experience</a></div>
<p>Spiritual experiences play a central role in modern spirituality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharf1995_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharf1995-42">[31]</a></sup> Both western and Asian authors have popularised this notion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHori199947_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHori199947-147">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERambachan1994_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERambachan1994-148">[128]</a></sup> Important early-20th century Western writers who studied the phenomenon of spirituality, and their works, include <a href="/wiki/William_James" title="William James">William James</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Varieties_of_Religious_Experience" title="The Varieties of Religious Experience">The Varieties of Religious Experience</a></i> (1902) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rudolph_Otto" title="Rudolph Otto">Rudolph Otto</a>, especially <i><a href="/wiki/The_Idea_of_the_Holy" title="The Idea of the Holy">The Idea of the Holy</a></i> (1917)
</p><p>James' notions of  "spiritual experience" had a further influence on the modernist streams in Asian traditions, making them even further recognisable for a western audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharf1995_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharf1995-42">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>William James popularized the use of the term "religious experience" in his <i>The Varieties of Religious Experience</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHori199947_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHori199947-147">[127]</a></sup> He has also influenced the understanding of mysticism as a distinctive experience which allegedly grants knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanford_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford-149">[web 11]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Wayne_Proudfoot" title="Wayne Proudfoot">Wayne Proudfoot</a> traces the roots of the notion of "religious experience" further back to the German theologian <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Schleiermacher" title="Friedrich Schleiermacher">Friedrich Schleiermacher</a> (1768–1834), who argued that religion is based on a <i>feeling</i> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Infinite" title="The Infinite">the infinite</a>. Schleiermacher used the idea of "religious experience" to defend religion against the growing scientific and secular critique. Many scholars of religion, of whom William James was the most influential, adopted the concept.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharf2000271_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharf2000271-150">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>Major Asian influences on contemporary spirituality have included <a href="/wiki/Swami_Vivekananda" title="Swami Vivekananda">Swami Vivekananda</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERenard2010191_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERenard2010191-151">[130]</a></sup> (1863–1902) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D.T._Suzuki" title="D.T. Suzuki">D.T. Suzuki</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharf1995_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharf1995-42">[31]</a></sup> (1870–1966) Vivekananda popularised a modern <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretic</a> Hinduism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESinari2000_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESinari2000-152">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERambachan1994_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERambachan1994-148">[128]</a></sup> in which an emphasis on personal experience replaced the authority of scriptures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERambachan1994_148-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERambachan1994-148">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComans1993_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComans1993-153">[132]</a></sup> Suzuki had a major influence on the popularisation of <a href="/wiki/Zen_in_the_United_States" title="Zen in the United States">Zen in the west</a> and popularized the idea of <a href="/wiki/Enlightenment_(spiritual)" title="Enlightenment (spiritual)">enlightenment</a> as insight into a timeless, transcendent reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[web 12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[web 13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcMahan2008_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcMahan2008-44">[32]</a></sup> Other influences came through <a href="/wiki/Paul_Brunton" title="Paul Brunton">Paul Brunton</a>'s <i>A Search in Secret India</i> (1934),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[133]</a></sup> which introduced <a href="/wiki/Ramana_Maharshi" title="Ramana Maharshi">Ramana Maharshi</a> (1879–1950) and <a href="/wiki/Meher_Baba" title="Meher Baba">Meher Baba</a> (1894–1969) to a western audience.
</p><p>Spiritual experiences can include being connected to a larger reality, yielding a more comprehensive <a href="/wiki/Self" title="Self">self</a>; joining with other individuals or the <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> <a href="/wiki/Community" title="Community">community</a>; with <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">nature</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Cosmos" title="Cosmos">cosmos</a>; or with the <a href="/wiki/Divinity" title="Divinity">divine</a> realm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spiritual_practices">Spiritual practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Spiritual practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spiritual_practice" title="Spiritual practice">Spiritual practice</a></div>
<p>Kees Waaijman discerns four forms of spiritual practices:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000644–645_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000644–645-158">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>Somatic practices, especially deprivation and diminishment. Deprivation aims to purify the body. Diminishment concerns the repulsement of ego-oriented impulses. Examples include fasting and poverty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000644–645_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000644–645-158">[135]</a></sup></li>
<li>Psychological practices, for example meditation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000645_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000645-159">[136]</a></sup></li>
<li>Social practices. Examples include the practice of obedience and communal ownership, reforming ego-orientedness into other-orientedness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000645_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000645-159">[136]</a></sup></li>
<li>Spiritual. All practices aim at purifying ego-centeredness, and direct the abilities at the divine reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000645_159-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaaijman2000645-159">[136]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>Spiritual practices may include <a href="/wiki/Meditation" title="Meditation">meditation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mindfulness" title="Mindfulness">mindfulness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prayer" title="Prayer">prayer</a>, the contemplation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacred_text" title="Sacred text">sacred texts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethic" title="Ethic">ethical</a> development,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dalai_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dalai-137">[117]</a></sup>
and spiritual retreats in a convent. Love and/or compassion are often<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="This term requires quantification. (December 2015)">quantify</span></a></i>]</sup> described<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (September 2019)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> as the mainstay of spiritual development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dalai_137-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dalai-137">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Within spirituality is also found "a common emphasis on the value of thoughtfulness, tolerance for breadth and practices and beliefs, and appreciation for the insights of other religious communities, as well as other sources of authority within the social sciences."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science">Science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_to_science">Relation to science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Relation to science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Conflict_thesis" title="Conflict thesis">Conflict thesis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Relationship_between_religion_and_science" title="Relationship between religion and science">Relationship between religion and science</a></div>
<p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Science_in_the_Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Science in the Age of Enlightenment">scientific revolution</a> of the 18th-century Enlightenment, the relationship of science to religion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SandR_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-SandR-162">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[140]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (March 2018)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and to spirituality<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> has developed in complex ways. Historian <a href="/wiki/John_Hedley_Brooke" title="John Hedley Brooke">John Hedley Brooke</a> describes wide variations:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The natural sciences have been invested with religious meaning, with antireligious implications and, in many contexts, with no religious significance at all."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[141]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Brooke has proposed that the currently held popular notion of antagonisms between science and religion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[143]</a></sup> has historically originated with "thinkers with a social or political axe to grind" rather than with the natural philosophers themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[144]</a></sup> Though physical and biological scientists today see no need for <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> explanations to describe reality<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[146]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (March 2018)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[note 11]</a></sup>, some<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="This term requires quantification. (March 2018)">quantify</span></a></i>]</sup> scientists continue to regard science and spirituality as complementary, not contradictory,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[149]</a></sup> and are willing to debate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[150]</a></sup>
rather than simply classifying spirituality and science as <a href="/wiki/Non-overlapping_magisteria" title="Non-overlapping magisteria">non-overlapping magisteria</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/William_James" title="William James">William James</a>, one of the preeminent philosophers of religious experience &amp; spirituality termed the general critique of religious experience by <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">Science</a> as the "Survival Theory". He writes, "There is a notion in the air about us that religion is probably only an anachronism, a case of 'survival,' an atavistic relapse into a mode of thought which humanity in its more enlightened examples has outgrown; and this notion our religious anthropologists at present do little to counteract".<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJames1901–1902439"Lecture_XX:_Conclusions"_175-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJames1901–1902439"Lecture_XX:_Conclusions"-175'>[151]</a></sup> He makes the claim that theology, or the science of religion will never truly understand its subject matter,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (July 2022)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> "just as <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Al Ghazzali</a> told us [...] that to understand the causes of drunkenness, as a physician understands them, is not to be drunk. A science may come to understand everything about the causes and elements of religion, and might even decide which elements were qualified, by their general harmony with other branches of knowledge, to be considered true; and yet the best man at this science might be the man who found it hardest to be personally devout".<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJames1901–1902438"Lecture_XX:_Conclusions"_176-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJames1901–1902438"Lecture_XX:_Conclusions"-176'>[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>A few<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="This term requires quantification. (March 2018)">quantify</span></a></i>]</sup> religious leaders have shown openness to modern science and its methods. The 14th <a href="/wiki/Dalai_Lama" title="Dalai Lama">Dalai Lama</a>, for example, has proposed that if a scientific analysis conclusively showed certain claims in Buddhism to be false, then the claims must be abandoned and the findings of science accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantum_mysticism">Quantum mysticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Quantum mysticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the twentieth century the relationship between science and spirituality has been influenced both by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freud" title="Freud">Freudian</a> psychology, which has accentuated the boundaries between the two areas by accentuating <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">individualism</a> and secularism, and by developments in <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a>, which reopened the debate about <a href="/wiki/Relationship_between_religion_and_science" title="Relationship between religion and science">complementarity</a> between scientific and religious discourse and rekindled for many an interest in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holistic" title="Holistic">holistic</a> conceptions of reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SandR_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-SandR-162">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 322">: 322 </span></sup> These conceptions were championed by New Age spiritualists in a type of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mysticism" title="Quantum mysticism">quantum mysticism</a> that they claim justifies their spiritual beliefs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Capra_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Capra-178">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[155]</a></sup> though <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum physicists</a> themselves on the whole reject such attempts as being <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscientific</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_research">Scientific research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Scientific research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_and_well-being">Health and well-being</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Health and well-being">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_and_health" title="Religion and health">Religion and health</a></div>
<p>Various studies (most  originating from North America) have reported a positive <a href="/wiki/Correlation" title="Correlation">correlation</a> between spirituality and mental well-being in both healthy people and those encountering a range of physical illnesses or psychological disorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoenig2008_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoenig2008-185">[161]</a></sup> Although spiritual individuals tend to be optimistic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2019_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2019-186">[162]</a></sup> report greater social support,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[163]</a></sup> and experience higher intrinsic <a href="/wiki/Meaning_of_life" title="Meaning of life">meaning in life</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[164]</a></sup> strength, and <a href="/wiki/Inner_peace" title="Inner peace">inner peace</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[165]</a></sup> whether the correlation represents a causal link remains contentious. Both supporters and opponents of this claim agree that past statistical findings are difficult to interpret, in large part because of the ongoing disagreement over how spirituality should be defined and measured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2013_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2013-144">[124]</a></sup> There is also evidence that an agreeable/positive temperament and/or a tendency toward sociability (which all correlate with spirituality) might actually be the key psychological features that predispose people to subsequently adopt a spiritual orientation and that these characteristics, not spiritually <i>per se</i>, add to well-being. There is also some suggestion that the benefits associated with spirituality and religiosity might arise from being a member of a close-knit community. Social bonds available via secular sources (i.e.,  not unique to spirituality or faith-based groups) might just as effectively raise well-being. In sum, spirituality may not be the "active ingredient" (i.e., past association with psychological well-being measures might reflect a reverse causation or effects from other variables that correlate with spirituality),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2011_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2011-138">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2013a_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2013a-190">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2010_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2010-193">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[171]</a></sup> and that the effects of agreeableness, conscientiousness, or virtue – personality traits common in many non-spiritual people yet known to be slightly more common among the spiritual – may better account for spirituality's apparent correlation with mental health and social support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2011_138-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2011-138">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2017_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2017-197">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[175]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Intercessionary_prayer">Intercessionary prayer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Intercessionary prayer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Masters and Spielmans<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[176]</a></sup> conducted a <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">meta-analysis</a> of all the available and reputable research examining the effects of distant <a href="/wiki/Intercession" title="Intercession">intercessory</a> prayer.  They found no discernible health effects from being prayed for by others. In fact, one large and scientifically rigorous study by Herbert Benson and colleagues<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[177]</a></sup> revealed that intercessory prayer had no effect on recovery from cardiac arrest, but patients told people were praying for them actually had an <i>increased risk</i> of medical complications.  Knowing others are praying for you could actually be medically detrimental.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spiritual_care_in_health_care_professions">Spiritual care in health care professions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Spiritual care in health care professions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spiritual_care_in_health_care_professions" title="Spiritual care in health care professions">Spiritual care in health care professions</a></div>
<p>In the health-care professions there is growing<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="This term requires quantification. (July 2017)">quantify</span></a></i>]</sup> interest in "spiritual care", to complement the medical-technical approaches and to improve the outcomes of medical treatments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoenigKingCarson2012_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoenigKingCarson2012-202">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuchalskiVitilloHullRelle2014_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuchalskiVitilloHullRelle2014-203">[179]</a></sup> Puchalski et al. argue for "<a href="/wiki/Compassion" title="Compassion">compassionate</a> systems of care" in a spiritual context.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spiritual_experiences">Spiritual experiences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Spiritual experiences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Neuroscientist" title="Neuroscientist">Neuroscientists</a> have examined brain functioning during reported spiritual experiences<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[181]</a></sup> finding that certain neurotransmitters and specific areas of the brain are involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[185]</a></sup>  Moreover, experimenters have also successfully induced spiritual experiences in individuals by administering <a href="/wiki/Psychoactive_drug" title="Psychoactive drug">psychoactive agents</a> known to elicit euphoria and perceptual distortions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[187]</a></sup> Conversely, religiosity and spirituality can also be <i>dampened</i> by electromagnetic stimulation of the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[188]</a></sup> These results have motivated some leading theorists to speculate that spirituality may be a benign subtype of <a href="/wiki/Psychosis" title="Psychosis">psychosis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2013a_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchuurmans-Stekhoven2013a-190">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[web 14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cottam,_S._2011_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cottam,_S._2011-215">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies,_M.F._2001_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies,_M.F._2001-216">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[192]</a></sup> – benign in the sense that the same aberrant sensory perceptions that those suffering clinical psychoses evaluate as distressingly incongruent and inexplicable are instead interpreted by spiritual individuals as positive (personal and meaningful transcendent experiences).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cottam,_S._2011_215-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cottam,_S._2011-215">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies,_M.F._2001_216-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davies,_M.F._2001-216">[191]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement">Measurement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Measurement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Considerable debate persists about — among other factors — spirituality's relation to religion, the number and content of its dimensions, its relation to concepts of well-being, and its universality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MAC_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-MAC-218">[193]</a></sup> (ref) A number of research groups have developed instruments which attempt to measure spirituality quantitatively, including the Spiritual Transcendence Scale (STS), the Brief Multidimensional Measure of Religiousness/Spirituality (BMMRS) and the Daily Spiritual Experiences Scale. MacDonald et al. gave an "Expressions of Spirituality Inventory" (ESI-R) measuring five dimensions of spirituality to over 4000 persons across eight countries. The study results and interpretation highlighted the complexity and challenges of measurement of spirituality cross-culturally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MAC_218-1"><a href="#cite_note-MAC-218">[193]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-106"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-106">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
<a href="/wiki/Klaus_Klostermaier" title="Klaus Klostermaier">Klaus Klostermaier</a> discusses examples from Bhagavata Purana, another ancient Hindu scripture, where a forest worker discovers observing mother nature is a spiritual practice, to wisdom and liberating knowledge. The Purana suggests that "true knowledge of nature" leads to "true knowledge of Self and God." It illustrates 24 gurus that nature provides. For example, earth teaches steadfastness and the wisdom that all things while pursuing their own activities, do nothing but follow the divine laws that are universally established; another wisdom from earth is her example of accepting the good and bad from everyone. Another guru, the honeybee teaches that one must make effort to gain knowledge, a willingness and flexibility to examine, pick and collect essence from different scriptures and sources. And so on. Nature is a mirror image of spirit, perceptive awareness of nature can be spirituality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[87]</a></sup></span>
</li>

</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spirituality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarrollO'ConnorMeakes200544-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarrollO'ConnorMeakes200544_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarrollO'ConnorMeakes200544_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarrollO'ConnorMeakes200544_1-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMcCarrollO'ConnorMeakes2005">McCarroll, O'Connor &amp; Meakes 2005</a>, p. 44.</span>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoenigKingCarson201236-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoenigKingCarson201236_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoenigKingCarson201236_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoenigKingCarson201236_2-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFKoenigKingCarson2012">Koenig, King &amp; Carson 2012</a>, p. 36.</span>

<li id="cite_note-134"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-134">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
